## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |

| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of github.com/sg-evals)/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the abili

     - Indexer (in-process cache)
     - SharedInformer (event multiplexing)
     - Factory (lifecycle management)

   - Resync performance implications

Input
{
  "path": "staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md",
  "repo": "github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0"
}
Output
{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",

  }

An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n50: //\\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n55: //\\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n68: // selector.\\n69: //\\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n76: //\\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n78: // during `Run()`.\\n79: //\\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n87: //\\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n94: //\\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n99: // into its constituent parts.\\n100: //\\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n104: //\\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n130: //\\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n133: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n134: // to something else, for example through a\\n135: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n136: //\\n137: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n138: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n139: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n140: type SharedInformer interface {\\n141: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n142: \\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\\n143: \\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\\n144: \\t// different handlers.\\n145: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\\n146: \\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\\n147: \\t// seen every item in the initial list).\\n148: \\t//\\n149: \\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\\n150: \\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\\n151: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/shared_informer.go%3FL1-150\"}",

    "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: package cache\n18: \n19: import (\n20: \t\"context\"\n21: \t\"errors\"\n22: \t\"fmt\"\n23: \t\"sync\"\n24: \t\"sync/atomic\"\n25: \t\"time\"\n26: \n27: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/api/meta\"\n28: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/runtime\"\n29: \tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n30: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/wait\"\n31: \t\"k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/synctrack\"\n32: \t\"k8s.io/utils/buffer\"\n33: \t\"k8s.io/utils/clock\"\n34: \t\"k8s.io/utils/ptr\"\n35: \n36: \t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n37: \n38: \tclientgofeaturegate \"k8s.io/client-go/features\"\n39: )\n40: \n41: // SharedInformer provides eventually consistent linkage of its\n42: // clients to the authoritative state of a given collection of\n43: // objects.  An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n50: //\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n55: //\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n68: // selector.\n69: //\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\n76: //\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n78: // during `Run()`.\n79: //\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\n87: //\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n94: //\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n99: // into its constituent parts.\n100: //\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n104: //\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n130: //\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n133: // notifications to deliver. {
  "content": "# Client-Go Systems Guide: Advanced Architecture and Extension Patterns\n\n## Overview and Subsystem Boundaries\n\n**client-go** is the official Kubernetes Go client library, providing both programmatic access to the Kubernetes API and the foundational event-driven, cached machinery for building controllers. It sits between application code and the Kubernetes API server, abstracting both the complexity of HTTP serialization/deserialization and the low-level patterns needed for efficient resource watching.\n\n### Upstream Dependencies\n- **k8s.io/apimachinery**: Core runtime types, serialization, watch events, errors\n- **k8s.io/api**: API type definitions (core v1, apps, batch, etc.)\n- **net/http**: Go standard library for HTTP transport\n- **context**: Go context for cancellation and deadlines\n\n### Downstream Consumers\n- Controllers (kube-controller-manager, kubelet, scheduler)\n- Client applications (kubectl, custom operators)\n- Testing frameworks and mock implementations\n- Plugin systems (auth providers, exec-based authentication)\n\n---\n\n## Core Components and Responsibility Boundaries\n\n### 1. REST Layer (`rest/` package)\n\n**Location**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/`\n\nThe REST layer provides low-level HTTP machinery that all higher-level clients ultimately use. It is responsible for:\n\n- **Config management** (`rest/config.go`): Holds connection parameters, authentication credentials, TLS settings, rate limits, and timeouts. The `Config` struct is the normalized in-memory representation of kubeconfig data, abstracting away file parsing concerns.\n\n- **Request building** (`rest/request.go`): Implements a fluent builder pattern for constructing Kubernetes API requests. Each request path element (verb, resource, namespace, etc.) is added via chained method calls like `.Get().Resource(\"pods\").Namespace(\"default\").Name(\"nginx\")`. This defers actual execution until a terminal method like `.Into(&obj)` is called.\n\n- **Transport layer** (`rest/transport.go`): Converts Config into a chain of `http.RoundTripper` middleware. Each middleware handles a specific concern: TLS certificate loading, bearer token injection, impersonation headers, basic auth, and per-request timeout enforcement.\n\n```go\n// Example Config fields (rest/config.go, lines 55-165)\ntype Config struct {\n    Host        string\n    APIPath     string\n    BearerToken string\n    QPS         float32     // Default 5.0\n    Burst       int         // Default 10\n    Transport   http.RoundTripper\n    WrapTransport transport.WrapperFunc\n    // ... auth, TLS, proxy, timeout fields\n}\n```\n\n**Key design decisions**:\n- Builder pattern separates request construction from execution, allowing code generation to produce type-safe wrappers without duplicating serialization logic\n- RoundTripper chain is the primary extension point for authentication and custom middleware\n- Content negotiation via Accept headers enables clients to request metadata-only objects (`as=PartialObjectMetadata`) or tables (`as=Table`), improving performance\n\n### 2. Typed Client (`kubernetes/` package)\n\n**Location**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/`\n\nThe Clientset provides compile-time type safety by grouping resources by API group and version. It is generated via `code-generator` from API type definitions.\n\n```go\n// kubernetes/clientset.go structure (lines 83-137)\ntype Interface interface {\n    Discovery() discovery.DiscoveryInterface\n    AppsV1() appsv1.AppsV1Interface\n    CoreV1() corev1.CoreV1Interface\n    BatchV1() batchv1.BatchV1Interface\n    // ... methods for all API groups and versions\n}\n\ntype Clientset struct {\n    *discovery.DiscoveryClient\n    appsV1        *appsv1.AppsV1Client\n    coreV1        *corev1.CoreV1Client\n    batchV1       *batchv1.BatchV1Client\n    // ... fields for all API groups\n}\n```\n\nEach group version interface (e.g., `AppsV1Interface`) provides access to a set of resource interfaces, each of which supports standard CRUD operations and subresources. For example:\n\n```go\n// Example usage pattern\nclientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\"default\").Create(ctx, deployment, metav1.CreateOptions{})\nclientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\"default\").Watch(ctx, metav1.ListOptions{})\n```\n\n**Responsibility**:\n- Presents strongly typed, method-based API for built-in resources\n- Encapsulates URL construction and serialization details\n- Enables static type checking and IDE autocomplete\n\n### 3. Dynamic Client (`dynamic/` package)\n\n**Location**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/`\n\nThe dynamic client handles resources unknown at compile time (notably CRDs) by representing all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured` (essentially a map of fields).\n\n```go\n// dynamic/interface.go (lines 29-50)\ntype Interface interface {\n    Resource(resource schema.GroupVersionResource) NamespaceableResourceInterface\n}\n\ntype ResourceInterface interface {\n    Create(ctx context.Context, obj *unstructured.Unstructured, ...) (*unstructured.Unstructured, error)\n    Get(ctx context.Context, name string, ...) (*unstructured.Unstructured, error)\n    List(ctx context.Context, opts metav1.ListOptions) (*unstructured.UnstructuredList, error)\n    Watch(ctx context.Context, opts metav1.ListOptions) (watch.Interface, error)\n    Patch(ctx context.Context, name string, pt types.PatchType, data []byte, ...) (*unstructured.Unstructured, error)\n}\n```\n\n**Coordination with discovery**:\nThe dynamic client depends on the `DiscoveryClient` to determine what resources exist and their API paths. Metadata about resource structure comes from OpenAPI schemas fetched from `/openapi/v3`.\n\n### 4. Discovery Client (`discovery/` package)\n\n**Location**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/discovery/`\n\nThe discovery client introspects the API server to learn what API groups, versions, and resources it supports. This is essential for dynamic clients and tools like kubectl.\n\n**Key interfaces** (`discovery/discovery_client.go`, lines 77-155):\n\n```go\ntype DiscoveryInterface interface {\n    RESTClient() restclient.Interface\n    ServerGroupsInterface\n    ServerResourcesInterface\n    ServerVersionInterface\n    OpenAPISchemaInterface\n    OpenAPIV3SchemaInterface\n    WithLegacy() DiscoveryInterface\n}\n\ntype ServerResourcesInterface interface {\n    ServerResourcesForGroupVersion(groupVersion string) (*metav1.APIResourceList, error)\n    ServerGroupsAndResources() ([]*metav1.APIGroup, []*metav1.APIResourceList, error)\n    ServerPreferredResources() ([]*metav1.APIResourceList, error)\n}\n```\n\n**Aggregated discovery** (v2 format): Recent versions support `/api/v1/groups` endpoint returning `APIGroupDiscoveryList`, which combines groups and resources in a single request, reducing overhead.\n\n**CachedDiscoveryClient**: A wrapper that disk-caches discovery data (by default in `~/.kube/cache/discovery/`), improving startup time. The cache must be explicitly invalidated via `.Invalidate()` if stale data is detected.\n\n### 5. Informer Machinery (`tools/cache/` package)\n\n**Location**: `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/`\n\nThe informer is the core of client-go's event-driven architecture. It replaces a polling-heavy pattern with an efficient, cached, watch-based model.\n\n#### 5.1 Reflector (`tools/cache/reflector.go`)\n\nThe reflector is the bridge between the API server and the local cache. It performs two operations in sequence:\n\n1. **Initial LIST**: Fetches all objects of a resource type, capturing the `resourceVersion` at that moment\n2. **Continuous WATCH**: Streams changes from that `resourceVersion` forward\n\n```go\n// reflector.go (lines 87-148)\ntype Reflector struct {\n    name            string\n    expectedType    reflect.Type\n    store           ReflectorStore       // Receives updates\n    listerWatcher   ListerWatcherWithContext\n    resyncPeriod    time.Duration\n    lastSyncResourceVersion string\n    // ...\n}\n\nfunc (r *Reflector) ListAndWatchWithContext(ctx context.Context) error {\n    // Either watchList or traditional list+watch\n    if r.useWatchList {\n        w, err = r.watchList(ctx)\n        // ...\n    } else {\n        err = r.list(ctx)\n        // ...\n    }\n    return r.watchWithResync(ctx, w)\n}\n```\n\n**Resilience mechanisms**:\n- **Exponential backoff** on watch failures (800ms initial, up to 30s)\n- **Watch bookmarks**: Server-sent bookmark events provide updated `resourceVersion` when the connection is stale, allowing recovery without full re-list\n- **\"Too old\" resource version handling**: If a WATCH fails because the requested `resourceVersion` is too old (server-side history window exceeded), the reflector automatically re-lists and resumes watching\n- **Resync**: Periodic full-cache traversal (configurable via `resyncPeriod`) to catch mutations the watch stream may have missed due to server-side crashes or network partitions\n\n#### 5.2 DeltaFIFO (`tools/cache/delta_fifo.go`)\n\nBuffers changes before they reach the cache, preserving ordering and deduplication semantics. Each item in the FIFO is associated with a slice of `DeltaType` events (Added, Modified, Deleted, Sync). If an object is modified multiple times before being popped, only the final state is forwarded to the cache.\n\n#### 5.3 Indexer (Thread-Safe Store) (`tools/cache/store.go`, `tools/cache/index.go`)\n\nThe indexer is the in-process cache. It stores objects keyed by namespace and name (e.g., `\"default/nginx\"`), and supports secondary indices for efficient lookups (e.g., by owner reference or label).\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/listers.go (lines 1-50)\n// Lister is the thread-safe, read-only interface to the cache\ntype Lister interface {\n    List(selector labels.Selector) (ret []interface{}, err error)\n    Get(name string) (ret interface{}, err error)\n}\n```\n\n#### 5.4 SharedInformer (`tools/cache/shared_informer.go`)\n\nMultiplexes a single Reflector+DeltaFIFO+Indexer across many event handler subscribers. Without SharedInformer, each handler would need its own watch stream, overwhelming the API server.\n\n```go\n// tools/cache/shared_informer.go (lines 140-150)\ntype SharedInformer interface {\n    AddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n    GetStore() Store\n    GetIndexer() Indexer\n    Run(stopCh <-chan struct{})\n    HasSynced() bool\n}\n\n// ResourceEventHandler receives cache events\ntype ResourceEventHandler interface {\n    OnAdd(obj interface{}, isInitialList bool)\n    OnUpdate(oldObj, newObj interface{})\n    OnDelete(obj interface{})\n}\n```\n\nEvent handlers are called in order, sequentially, **after** the cache has been updated. This ordering is critical for controller correctness: handlers never see stale cache data.\n\n#### 5.5 Informer Factory (`informers/factory.go`)\n\nFactory that creates and manages a family of typed SharedInformers for a single Clientset. Ensures each resource type has at most one Reflector, reducing API server load.\n\n```go\n// informers/factory.go (lines 54-112)\ntype SharedInformerFactory interface {\n    Start(stopCh <-chan struct{})\n    WaitForCacheSync(stopCh <-chan struct{}) map[reflect.Type]bool\n    Apps() apps.Interface      // Provides AppV1(), AppV1beta1(), etc.\n    Batch() batch.Interface\n    Core() core.Interface\n    // ...\n}\n\nfunc NewSharedInformerFactoryWithOptions(\n    client kubernetes.Interface,\n    defaultResync time.Duration,\n    options ...SharedInformerOption,\n) SharedInformerFactory {\n    // Supports WithNamespace(), WithTweakListOptions(), WithCustomResyncConfig(), WithTransform()\n}\n```\n\n---\n\n## End-to-End Control Flow: From Event to Reconciliation\n\n### Scenario: A Deployment is updated\n\n```\nAPI Server (persistent state)\n    \u2193\n[1] Reflector.watchList() or list() + watch()\n    \u2193\n[2] Watch event (MODIFIED) streamed to reflector\n    \u2193\n[3] Reflector sends object to DeltaFIFO.Add()\n    \u2193\n[4] DeltaFIFO internal goroutine pops item, sends to Indexer\n    \u2193\n[5] Indexer updates cache, calls SharedInformer.OnUpdate()\n    \u2193\n[6] SharedInformer dispatches to registered event handlers\n    \u2193\n[7] Event handler calls: workqueue.Add(\"deployment/default/my-deploy\")\n    \u2193\n[8] Controller.Run() calls: item, _ := workqueue.Get()\n    \u2193\n[9] Controller reconcile loop:\n    - Fetch from cache: lister.Get(\"default/my-deploy\")\n    - Perform business logic (e.g., check replica count)\n    - Issue API calls if needed (e.g., patch status)\n    - workqueue.Done(item)\n    \u2193\n[10] If error, workqueue re-enqueues with exponential backoff\n```\n\n### File evidence of flow:\n\n1. **Reflector start**: `tools/cache/reflector.go:364-373` (`RunWithContext`)\n2. **List and watch**: `tools/cache/reflector.go:408-447` (`ListAndWatchWithContext`)\n3. **Watch stream handling**: `tools/cache/reflector.go:499-700+` (`watch()` method processes events)\n4. **DeltaFIFO queuing**: `tools/cache/delta_fifo.go` (internal Add/Update/Delete feed the FIFO)\n5. **Indexer update**: `tools/cache/store.go` (cache population after FIFO pops)\n6. **Event handler dispatch**: `tools/cache/shared_informer.go` (processor listeners triggered post-cache-update)\n7. **Work queue integration**: `util/workqueue/queue.go:30-52` (TypedInterface for Add/Get/Done)\n\n### Code Generation Integration\n\nTyped informers and listers are generated via `k8s.io/code-generator`. The generation process creates:\n- **Typed Clientsets** (e.g., `kubernetes.Clientset`)\n- **Typed Informers** (e.g., `v1.PodInformer`)\n- **Typed Listers** (e.g., `v1.PodLister`) \u2014 read-only cache accessors\n- **Apply configurations** (e.g., `v1.DeploymentApplyConfiguration`) for Server-Side Apply\n\nFor the Kubernetes repository itself, code generation is invoked via `hack/update-codegen.sh`.\n\n---\n\n## Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 1. Watch Stream Disconnection\n\n**Symptom**: Reflector logs \"Failed to watch\" or \"error(conn-refused)\"\n\n**Recovery**:\n- Exponential backoff triggers `ListAndWatch` again\n- Full re-list ensures no gap in cache (though potentially stale)\n- Watch bookmarks (if supported) help resume from a recent resourceVersion without full re-list\n\n**Tradeoff**: Re-listing on every watch failure is expensive but guarantees eventual consistency. Without it, the cache could permanently miss objects.\n\n### 2. Resource Version Too Old\n\n**Symptom**: Watch fails with `410 Gone` (resource version expired on server)\n\n**Cause**: Etcd history (default 5 minutes) has been garbage collected; the client's cached `resourceVersion` is too old.\n\n**Recovery** (`reflector.go:120-122`):\n```go\nisLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable bool  // Set to true on 410 error\n```\nNext `ListAndWatch` cycle performs a full re-list to get a current resource version.\n\n**Limit**: If a controller is paused for longer than the server's history window, it **will** miss intermediate states. Controllers should be designed to be idempotent and re-reconcile all objects periodically (via resync).\n\n### 3. Event Handler Blocking\n\n**Symptom**: Watch stream stalls, reflector unable to process events\n\n**Root cause**: Event handler (or downstream workqueue) blocks, holding the lock in `SharedInformer`.\n\n**Mitigation** (`tools/cache/shared_informer.go:131-135`):\n- Event handlers must complete quickly\n- Long-running work should be delegated to a workqueue consumer\n- Handlers run sequentially; blocking one handler blocks all others for the same informer\n\n**Best practice**: Handlers should only call `workqueue.Add(key)` and return immediately.\n\n### 4. High-Frequency Resync\n\n**Symptom**: CPU spike, API server load spike\n\n**Cause**: `resyncPeriod` is too small (e.g., 1 second instead of the default 0 or 30s).\n\n**Impact**:\n- Full cache traverse every N seconds\n- Calls `Indexer.Resync()` which re-triggers all event handlers\n- Can be amplified across many controllers\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use sensible defaults (0 = no resync, or 30s+ for slow-changing resources)\n- Set via `informers/factory.go:77-85` (`WithCustomResyncConfig`)\n\n### 5. List Pagination and Consistency\n\n**Feature** (Reflector `WatchListPageSize`):\n- Paginated LIST requests (configurable chunk size) reduce per-request memory on both client and server\n- **Design tradeoff**: Paginated lists are served directly from etcd, bypassing cache, so they are slower than watch cache serves\n\n**Default behavior**:\n- Consistent reads (RV=\"\") or old reads (RV=\"0\"): uses paging\n- Specific resourceVersion reads: disables pagination to use watch cache\n\n### 6. Server-Side vs. Client-Side Rate Limiting\n\n**Client-side rate limiting** (`rest/config.go:117-129`):\n- `QPS` (default 5.0) and `Burst` (default 10) control client's token bucket\n- Prevents overwhelming the server\n\n**Server-side rate limiting** (API Priority and Fairness):\n- Server enforces priority classes and flow control\n- Client handles HTTP 429 with exponential backoff (Retry-After header)\n\n**Interaction**:\n- If client-side QPS/Burst are too high, server will throttle and client will backoff\n- If too low, workload is unnecessarily slow\n- Default values are conservative; set based on actual workload and API server capacity\n\n---\n\n## Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 1. Custom Transport Middleware\n\n**Location**: `rest/config.go` and `rest/transport.go`\n\n```go\nconfig := &rest.Config{\n    Host: \"https://...\",\n    // ... auth, TLS, etc.\n    WrapTransport: func(rt http.RoundTripper) http.RoundTripper {\n        return &customMiddleware{rt: rt}\n    },\n}\n```\n\nUse cases:\n- Request/response logging and tracing\n- Custom retry logic or circuit breaking\n- Request header injection (e.g., tracing context, custom authentication)\n- Rate limiting per endpoint\n\n**Risk**: Broken middleware can cause all API calls to fail silently. Test thoroughly.\n\n### 2. Custom ListerWatcher\n\n**Location**: `tools/cache/reflector.go:104-105`\n\n```go\nlw := &cache.ListWatch{\n    ListFunc: func(options metav1.ListOptions) (runtime.Object, error) {\n        // Custom list logic, e.g., from cache, database, or filtered API call\n    },\n    WatchFunc: func(options metav1.ListOptions) (watch.Interface, error) {\n        // Custom watch, e.g., synthetic watch from polling\n    },\n}\n\nreflector := cache.NewReflector(lw, &v1.Pod{}, store, resyncPeriod)\n```\n\nUse cases:\n- **Filtering at source**: Reduce cache size by pre-filtering in ListFunc\n- **Non-API sources**: Watch resources stored in a database or cache\n- **Test mocks**: Deterministic watch streams for testing controller logic\n\n**Risk**: If ListFunc and WatchFunc are inconsistent, the cache can drift from truth. Ensure both serve the same semantics.\n\n### 3. Custom Indexers\n\n**Location**: `tools/cache/index.go`\n\n```go\nindexers := cache.Indexers{\n    cache.NamespaceIndex: cache.MetaNamespaceIndexFunc,\n    \"byOwnerRef\": func(obj interface{}) ([]string, error) {\n        pod := obj.(*v1.Pod)\n        var owners []string\n        for _, ref := range pod.OwnerReferences {\n            owners = append(owners, ref.UID)\n        }\n        return owners, nil\n    },\n}\nindexer := cache.NewIndexer(cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc, indexers)\n```\n\nUse cases:\n- Fast lookups by non-primary attributes (e.g., by owner, by label, by field)\n- Reduces O(n) scans to indexed lookups\n\n**Trade-off**: Index maintenance overhead vs. lookup speed. Most applications only need the default namespace/name index.\n\n### 4. Transform Function on Cache Items\n\n**Location**: `informers/factory.go:103-109` (`WithTransform`)\n\n```go\nfactory := informers.NewSharedInformerFactoryWithOptions(\n    client, 30*time.Second,\n    informers.WithTransform(func(obj interface{}) (interface{}, error) {\n        pod := obj.(*v1.Pod)\n        // Remove sensitive fields, apply annotations, etc.\n        return pod, nil\n    }),\n)\n```\n\nUse case:\n- Strip sensitive data before storing in cache\n- Normalize objects on ingestion\n\n**Risk**: If transform is lossy, controllers cannot recover the original data.\n\n### 5. Custom Event Handler with Lifecycle Hooks\n\n**Location**: `tools/cache/shared_informer.go:150-180`\n\n```go\ntype ResourceEventHandlerFuncs struct {\n    AddFunc    func(obj interface{}, isInitialList bool)\n    UpdateFunc func(oldObj, newObj interface{})\n    DeleteFunc func(obj interface{})\n}\n\n// Or use AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod for per-handler resync\ninformer.AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod(\n    &ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n        AddFunc: func(obj interface{}, _ bool) { workqueue.Add(keyOf(obj)) },\n        UpdateFunc: func(_, newObj interface{}) { workqueue.Add(keyOf(newObj)) },\n        DeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) { workqueue.Add(keyOf(obj)) },\n    },\n    30*time.Second, // per-handler resync (if non-zero)\n)\n```\n\n**Lifecycle semantics**:\n- `isInitialList=true` on initial sync, `false` on all subsequent updates\n- Delete notifications carry the **last known state** (except resourceVersion is updated to reflect deletion)\n- Sequential delivery: one handler's output doesn't run until previous handlers finish\n\n### 6. Server-Side Apply Configuration (Declarative Patching)\n\n**Location**: `applyconfigurations/` (generated), `util/apply/` package\n\n```go\n// Instead of get-modify-update:\ndeployment := applyconfigurations.Deployment(\"default\", \"my-deploy\").\n    WithSpec(applyconfigurations.DeploymentSpec().\n        WithReplicas(3).\n        WithSelector(...))\n\nresult, err := clientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\"default\").\n    Apply(ctx, deployment, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-controller\"})\n```\n\n**Advantages**:\n- Multiple controllers can safely co-manage the same object (via field ownership)\n- No race conditions between get and update\n- Server handles conflict resolution\n\n**Extension point**: Field managers are arbitrary strings; controllers declare ownership by FieldManager name.\n\n### 7. AuthProvider and ExecProvider Plugins\n\n**Location**: `rest/config.go:85-92`, `rest/plugin.go`\n\n```go\nconfig := &rest.Config{\n    // ...\n    AuthProvider: &clientcmdapi.AuthProviderConfig{\n        Name: \"oidc\",\n        Config: map[string]string{\n            \"client-id\": \"...\",\n            \"client-secret\": \"...\",\n        },\n    },\n}\n\n// Or use exec-based auth\nconfig.ExecProvider = &clientcmdapi.ExecConfig{\n    APIVersion: \"client.authentication.k8s.io/v1\",\n    Command: \"/path/to/auth-script\",\n}\n```\n\n**Lifecycle**:\n- Auth providers are loaded lazily on first API call\n- ExecProvider is invoked per-request if token is expired\n- WrapTransport is the mechanism for injecting auth headers\n\n**Extension paths**: Custom implementations via plugin interface.\n\n### 8. Custom Rate Limiter\n\n**Location**: `util/flowcontrol/`, `rest/config.go:128-129`\n\n```go\nimport \"k8s.io/client-go/util/flowcontrol\"\n\nlimiter := flowcontrol.NewTokenBucketRateLimiter(100, 10)\nconfig := &rest.Config{\n    // ...\n    RateLimiter: limiter,\n}\n```\n\nUse cases:\n- Adaptive rate limiting based on server response codes\n- Per-endpoint rate limits\n- Jitter to prevent thundering herd\n\n---\n\n## Operational Implications and Performance Considerations\n\n### 1. Memory Footprint\n\n**Cache Size**: Directly proportional to number of resources of each watched type.\n\n**Estimation**:\n- Typical Pod object: ~1-2 KB in memory\n- 10,000 Pods \u00d7 2 KB = ~20 MB cache\n- Add informer overhead (indices, event queues): 30-40 MB total\n\n**Optimization**:\n- Watch only needed resources (use `WithTweakListOptions` to filter)\n- Use `WithTransform` to strip large fields (e.g., logs, raw data)\n- Use metadata-only informer (`PartialObjectMetadata`) when full objects aren't needed\n\n### 2. API Server Load\n\n**Watch streams**: Each SharedInformer opens one WATCH request per resource type. With 10 controllers on a cluster, you have 10 Deployments watchers = 10 watch streams on the server.\n\n**Reflector resync**: Periodic full-cache traversal. If set too aggressively across many controllers, can spike API server CPU.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use SharedInformerFactory to share watchers across controllers\n- Set resync periods appropriately (default 0 or 30s+, not 1s)\n- Monitor watch stream count: `kube-apiserver_watch_cache_*` metrics\n\n### 3. Network Efficiency\n\n**Watch bookmarks** (`reflector.go:127-128`): Enable via feature gate `WatchListClient`. Reduces reconnection overhead by providing a recent resourceVersion on bookmark events.\n\n**List pagination** (`reflector.go:127-134`): Reduces per-request size on initial sync. Trade-off: slightly slower (served from etcd, not watch cache).\n\n**Protobuf encoding**:\n- Default JSON is ~1.5x larger than Protobuf\n- For high-volume scenarios (many objects, high QPS), consider `--prefers-protobuf` flag on generated clients\n\n### 4. CPU and GC Pressure\n\n**Reflector event processing**:\n- Allocates event objects per watch message\n- DeltaFIFO maintains linked list of deltas\n- Indexer maintains multiple indices\n\n**GC optimization**:\n- Ensure resync periods are not too aggressive\n- Limit cache size via filtering\n- Monitor heap profiling: `pprof /debug/pprof/heap`\n\n### 5. Latency: From Event to Controller Action\n\n**Typical end-to-end latency**:\n1. Object mutation on API server: 0 ms (reference point)\n2. WATCH stream delivery: 1-100 ms (network + serialization)\n3. Reflector processing: 1-10 ms\n4. DeltaFIFO \u2192 Indexer: <1 ms\n5. Event handler dispatch \u2192 workqueue.Add: <1 ms\n6. workqueue.Get: 0-1 ms (already queued)\n7. Controller reconcile: 10 ms - seconds (business logic)\n\n**Total**: Typically 20-100 ms from mutation to workqueue entry, assuming no backpressure.\n\n**Bottlenecks**:\n- Event handler blocking (blocks all handlers for that informer)\n- Workqueue consumer paused or slow (events accumulate)\n- Watch stream dropped (forces full re-list, expensive)\n\n### 6. Multi-Controller Scaling\n\n**Shared discovery cache**: Only one `CachedDiscoveryClient` is needed per kube-config. Multiple controllers can share it.\n\n**Shared informer factory**: Critical for efficiency. All controllers using the same SharedInformerFactory share a single Reflector per resource type.\n\n**Separate client vs. shared informer factory**:\n```go\n// Inefficient: two separate factories, two watch streams for Pods\nfactory1 := informers.NewSharedInformerFactory(clientset, 30*time.Second)\nfactory2 := informers.NewSharedInformerFactory(clientset, 30*time.Second)\npodInformer1 := factory1.Core().V1().Pods() // watch stream 1\npodInformer2 := factory2.Core().V1().Pods() // watch stream 2\n\n// Efficient: shared factory\nfactory := informers.NewSharedInformerFactory(clientset, 30*time.Second)\npodInformer1 := factory.Core().V1().Pods()  // same watch stream\npodInformer2 := factory.Core().V1().Pods()  // same watch stream\n```\n\n---\n\n## Important Files and Directory Map\n\n### Core REST and Transport\n- `rest/config.go` \u2014 Configuration struct and defaults\n- `rest/request.go` \u2014 Request builder and execution\n- `rest/transport.go` \u2014 Transport chain construction\n- `rest/with_retry.go` \u2014 Retry logic and backoff\n- `rest/warnings.go` \u2014 Warning header handling\n\n### Typed Clients\n- `kubernetes/clientset.go` \u2014 Generated Clientset struct\n- `kubernetes/typed/*/v1/client.go` \u2014 Typed client implementations (e.g., `typed/apps/v1/deployment.go`)\n\n### Dynamic Client\n- `dynamic/interface.go` \u2014 Dynamic client interfaces\n- `dynamic/simple.go` \u2014 Implementation using unstructured objects\n\n### Discovery\n- `discovery/discovery_client.go` \u2014 DiscoveryClient interface and implementation\n- `discovery/cached/` \u2014 CachedDiscoveryClient implementation\n- `discovery/helper.go` \u2014 Utility functions for discovery results\n\n### Informers and Cache\n- `tools/cache/reflector.go` \u2014 Reflector: LIST + WATCH + resync\n- `tools/cache/delta_fifo.go` \u2014 Delta FIFO queue\n- `tools/cache/store.go` \u2014 Cache store interface and default implementation\n- `tools/cache/index.go` \u2014 Indexing and secondary indices\n- `tools/cache/shared_informer.go` \u2014 SharedInformer: event multiplexing\n- `tools/cache/listers.go` \u2014 Generated lister interfaces (read-only cache accessors)\n\n### Informer Factory\n- `informers/factory.go` \u2014 SharedInformerFactory, coordinates all informers\n\n### Work Queue and Synchronization\n- `util/workqueue/queue.go` \u2014 Work queue interface and FIFO implementation\n- `util/workqueue/delaying_queue.go` \u2014 Delayed queue (for exponential backoff)\n- `util/workqueue/rate_limiting_queue.go` \u2014 Rate-limited queue (combines delaying + rate limiting)\n- `util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go` \u2014 Built-in rate limiters (exponential, linear backoff)\n\n### Tools and Utilities\n- `tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go` \u2014 Leader election machinery\n- `tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/` \u2014 Lock implementations (Lease, ConfigMap, etc.)\n- `tools/clientcmd/` \u2014 Kubeconfig parsing and client construction\n- `util/flowcontrol/` \u2014 Token bucket rate limiting\n\n### Code Generation\n- Referenced but not in client-go repo: `k8s.io/code-generator/` (external)\n- Output: clientset, informers, listers, apply configurations\n\n---\n\n## Conclusion\n\nClient-go's architecture achieves its goal of providing both ergonomic (typed client) and powerful (dynamic, watch-based, cached) interfaces to the Kubernetes API by layering abstractions:\n\n1. **REST layer**: Generic HTTP request builder with middleware chain for auth and transport\n2. **Typed and dynamic clients**: Build on REST layer, differing only in serialization strategy\n3. **Discovery**: Runtime introspection of API server capabilities\n4. **Informer machinery**: Event-driven caching with careful handling of watch disconnections, resource version expiry, and eventual consistency\n5. **Work queue and factories**: Coordinate multiple consumers of the same watch stream\n\nThe design emphasizes **resilience** (exponential backoff, bookmarks, re-sync), **efficiency** (single watch per resource type, cached reads), and **extensibility** (custom transport, ListerWatcher, indices, rate limiters). Controllers built with this library can scale from a single process to thousands of controllers per cluster without re-engineering the underlying machinery.\n",
